**Dagmál:** Sun. Wholeness. Vitality. The revelation that comes
with light and the health that comes with warmth. Sól is carved
on healing-stones and recovery beds and the east-facing posts of
every longhouse.

**Náttmál:** Total exposure. Life-force that burns what it
touches. Sól náttmál is the light that has no shadow — the
scrutiny that does not reveal truth but obliterates privacy. A
person under Sól náttmál cannot hide, cannot rest, cannot close
their eyes without the brightness finding them. Seiðr-wives use
Sól náttmál to force a spirit to manifest — the dead cannot hide
in the dark when the dark is burning — but the light damages
everything it touches, living and dead alike. There is a reason
the sun sets. Sól náttmál forgets that reason.

---

### Týr

**Dagmál:** Law. Sacrifice. Justice. The warrior's honor that
says _I will pay the price of what is right_. Týr is carved on
oath-stones and weapon-hilts and the pillars of every ting in the
Rimevegr.

**Náttmál:** Binding judgment. The law that has no mercy. Týr
náttmál carved on an oath-breaker's weapon means the weapon
turns — not physically, not visibly, but at the moment of crisis,
the blade finds the wrong target, the shield drops a heartbeat
too soon, the arm does not answer. Warriors who carry
Týr-cursed weapons do not die in battle. They die in the pause
after battle, when the adrenaline fades and they discover the
wound they did not feel and the wound is from their own blade.

---

### Bjarkan

**Dagmál:** Birch. Growth. Recovery. The green returning after
the Long Dark. Bjarkan is carved on cradles and sick-beds and
the posts of midwifery halls.

**Náttmál:** Growth that does not know when to stop. Recovery
that overshoots. Night-carved Bjarkan on a wound makes the
flesh close faster than it should, and the flesh that grows is
not quite right — too thick, too pale, too warm to the touch.
The seiðr-wives call Bjarkan náttmál healing _lífsvǫxtr_ (life-
overgrowth) and they warn against it: the body heals, but the
body changes, and the change is not reversible. A man healed
three times by Bjarkan náttmál has skin that moves independently
of the muscle beneath it, as though the skin has developed its
own intentions.

---

### Maður

**Dagmál:** Mankind. Community. The social order that separates
the human from the beast. Maður is carved on assembly-posts and
treaty-stones and the doors of halls where disputes are settled
by words instead of iron.

**Náttmál:** The crowd that turns. Public standing as a weapon.
Night-carved Maður on a man's property makes the community
forget him — not his name, not his face, but the instinct to
include him. He speaks and no one responds. He enters a room and
no one looks up. He calls for help and the sound arrives but the
urgency does not. Social erasure. The outlaws call it _dreaming
alive_ — being present in a room full of people who cannot
perceive that you are present. It is worse than exile because
exile is a decision. This is an oversight that never corrects.

---

### Eykur

**Dagmál:** Horse. Loyal motion. The partnership between rider
and mount that makes distance manageable. Eykur is carved on
saddles and harnesses and the gate-posts of steadings.

**Náttmál:** Momentum that cannot be stopped. The horse that
will not halt. Night-carved Eykur on a mount means the horse
runs and the rider cannot slow it and the destination is chosen
by the rune, not the rider. Three riders in the Frostmark
disappeared on Eykur-cursed horses. One was found two weeks
later, ninety miles north of his intended path, the horse dead
under him and his hands fused to the reins by dried blood and
cold. He could not explain where he had been. He could not
explain why the horse's eyes were open.

---

### Lögur

**Dagmál:** Water. Flow. Memory. The depth that holds what the
surface has forgotten. Lögur is carved on wells and boat-keels
and the stones placed at river-crossings.

**Náttmál:** Drowning memory. The current that pulls the mind
under. Night-carved Lögur on a drinking vessel makes the drinker
remember — not what they choose to remember, but what the water
chooses. The worst thing. The thing they buried. The face they
cannot look at. The sound of the ice breaking under someone they
loved. Lögur náttmál is used by seiðr-wives during interrogation:
a cup of water, a carved rune, and the prisoner drinks and weeps
and tells you everything because the memory will not stop until
the truth comes out.

---

### Ing

**Dagmál:** Fertility. Peace. Health. Safety. The blessing of a
household that is growing as it should. Ing is carved on
marriage-beds and birthing-stools and the hearth-stones of
halls where children are expected.

**Náttmál:** Safeguarded growth that serves the wrong master.
Night-carved Ing on a pregnant woman's threshold ensures the
child survives — ensures it with a ferocity that overrides
everything else. The mother may not survive. The household may
not survive. The child _will_, because the rune has decided, and
the rune's priority is the new life, and everything that is not
the new life is fuel. The midwives of the Rimevegr refuse to
carve Ing without daylight and steady hands. The stories of what
happens when Ing náttmál takes hold during a difficult birth are
not told in public.

---

### The Expansion Runes

Ten additional runes survive in fragments — barrow inscriptions,
sealed Skaldhaven records, the oral traditions of remote
settlements. They are not universally known. A galdr-worker may
know three or four of them. Nobody living knows all ten. Their
inclusion here is a reconstruction, assembled from multiple
sources, and some of the náttmál meanings are speculative.

---

### Nið

**Dagmál:** Shame. Disgrace. The public naming of what a man
has done wrong. Nið is the rune of social accountability — the
carved declaration that says _this person has failed and the
community knows_.

**Náttmál:** Social death. The shame that eats the name. Nið
náttmál carved on a man's door does not merely humiliate — it
unmakes his social existence. His name loses weight. His oaths
lose binding force. His word at the ting becomes noise. The
guðníðing described in Chapter 14 carve Nið into their own
flesh, deliberately, as the first step of the god-scorned path.
They are writing their own erasure.

---

### Hrafn

**Dagmál:** Raven. Omen. Battlefield intelligence. The bird that
sees the dead before the living know they have fallen. Hrafn is
carved on scout-staffs and message-poles.

**Náttmál:** Carrion-vision. The sight that cannot stop seeing
the dead. Night-carved Hrafn on a warrior's helm gives him the
raven's perspective — he sees the battlefield as a field of
meat, and the living are merely meat that has not stopped moving
yet. The sight persists after the battle ends. The warrior looks
at his companions and sees future corpses. He looks at children
and sees small corpses. The raven's gift is truth. The truth is
that everything alive is a body that does not yet know it is a
body. The sight fades in days. The knowledge does not.

---

### Blóð

**Dagmál:** Blood. Kinship. Sacrifice. The price paid in the
only currency the gods ever respected. Blóð is carved on
oath-rings and sacrifice-altars and the stones where blood-
brotherhood is sworn.

**Náttmál:** Blood-debt. The price that never finishes being
paid. Night-carved Blóð on a compact between two parties means
the betrayer bleeds — not metaphorically, not symbolically.
The wounds open without a blade. Small at first: nosebleeds,
bleeding gums, cuts that reopen. Then larger. A woman in the
Uppmark broke a Blóð-sealed trade agreement and bled from her
eyes for three days. She honored the agreement. The bleeding
stopped. She never trusted a galdr-worker again.

---

### Móðr

**Dagmál:** Fury. Courage. Battle-temper. The red heat that makes
a man stand when his body wants to fall. Móðr is the warrior's
rune, carved on weapon-hilts and shield-bosses.

**Náttmál:** Wrath without direction. The fury that does not
know friend from enemy. Night-carved Móðr on a warrior's weapon
makes him impossible to cow and impossible to control. The
berserker state — Úr for the body, Móðr for the spirit — is the
most feared rune-combination in the Rimevegr. The warriors who
carry both do not come back. Not dead. They come back _wrong_.
The fury does not leave when the battle ends. It lives in the
muscle memory. It lives in the dreams. It lives in the way the
man's hand tightens on the haft when someone raises their voice
in the hall.

---

### Mórk

**Dagmál:** Boundary. Marked land. Threshold. The line between
this and that, here and there, ours and theirs. Mórk is the
foundation of property law in the Rimevegr — carved on every
legal boundary-stone.

**Náttmál:** The boundary that seals. The edge that cuts. Night-
carved Mórk on a settlement's perimeter means the perimeter
becomes absolute — not a ward but a wall, not a defense but a
severance. Everything inside the boundary is one world.
Everything outside is another. The two do not communicate.
Messages do not cross. Sound does not carry. The settlement
exists in a pocket of silence, and the people inside do not
know the outside world still exists, and the outside world
forgets the settlement was ever there.

---

### Steinn

**Dagmál:** Stone. Permanence. The mound. What lasts when
everything else has rotted. Steinn is carved on grave-markers and
foundation-stones and the pillars of halls meant to stand for
generations.

**Náttmál:** The immovable curse. The weight that holds the
target in place. Night-carved Steinn on a man's boots means his
feet will not carry him from the spot where he stands. Not
paralysis — the muscles work, the joints bend — but the ground
will not release him. He pulls and the earth holds. Steinn
náttmál is used to anchor barrow-wards: the dead who are
Steinn-bound cannot rise because the stone remembers them as
stone and the stone will not let go. When Steinn-wards fail
after centuries, the draugr that emerge have skin like granite
and joints that crack like breaking rock.

---

### Salt

**Dagmál:** Preservation. Cleansing. Anti-corruption. Salt is
the rune of purity — the carving that keeps the rot at bay and
the boundary between clean and unclean intact.

**Náttmál:** Preservation without mercy. The refusal to let the
dead be dead. Night-carved Salt on a corpse keeps the body fresh
indefinitely — skin supple, eyes clear, no bloating, no stink.
The body looks asleep. It is not asleep. It is dead, perfectly
dead, and the perfection is obscene. Families have kept Salt-
preserved bodies for years, unable to grieve because the evidence
of death is absent. The rope-readers insist on burning any body
found with Salt náttmál carved into the burial wrappings. The
families resist. The argument never ends well.

---

### Mjǫðr

**Dagmál:** Mead. Poetry. Inspiration. The word-gift that
transforms speech into something that matters. Mjǫðr is the
skald's rune, carved on drinking-horns and speaker's posts.

**Náttmál:** Intoxicating insight. The truth that poisons the
speaker. Night-carved Mjǫðr gives the speaker eloquence that
comes from somewhere deeper than training — the words arrive
fully formed, devastating in their precision, impossible to
argue against. The speaker does not compose the speech. The
speech composes itself, using the speaker as a vessel. Afterward,
the speaker cannot remember what they said. Others can. Others
always remember. The speech changes them. It changes the
speaker too, but the speaker does not know how, because the
speech has erased the memory of its own delivery. Skaldhaven's
archive contains fourteen transcribed Mjǫðr-speeches. Reading
them is restricted. The restriction is not political. It is
medical.

---

### Skuggi

**Dagmál:** Shadow. Concealment. The ambiguity of things half-
seen. Skuggi is carved on thief-tools and spy-staffs and the
cloaks of men who need to be elsewhere.

**Náttmál:** Hidden identity. The doubled self. Night-carved
Skuggi on a man's cloak does not make him invisible — it makes
him _someone else_. Witnesses see a person. They describe a
person. The description is consistent and detailed and
completely wrong. The man they describe does not exist. Skuggi
náttmál is the forgery of presence — a false person layered over
the real one like frost over stone. The false person persists in
memory even after the rune is broken. Witnesses swear they saw
someone who was never born.

---

### Hlið

**Dagmál:** Gate. Passage. Threshold crossing. Hlið is the rune
of entry and exit — carved on door-frames and bridge-posts and
the entrance-stones of halls where guests are expected.

**Náttmál:** The sealed crossing. The door that opens one way.
Night-carved Hlið on a doorway means anyone may enter but no one
may leave — or anyone may leave but no one may enter, depending
on which face of the frame the rune is carved. Outlaws have used
Hlið náttmál to trap pursuit parties inside buildings: the door
opens inward, the pursuit enters, the door does not open outward.
The galdr-worker walks away. The building becomes a box.

---

### Mál

**Dagmál:** Speech. Measure. Legal speech. Agreement. Mál is the
rune of the lawspeaker — the spoken word that carries the weight
of decision.

**Náttmál:** Verdict without trial. The word that becomes law
because it was spoken in the right voice at the right time with
the right rune on the right post. Night-carved Mál on a ting-
stone means the first person to speak from that stone speaks
binding truth — not their opinion, not their argument, but the
underlying reality of the dispute, dragged into the open whether
the parties want it or not. Both sides leave destroyed. Justice
is served. Nobody is happy. This is what justice looks like in
the Rimevegr: a verdict that punishes everyone and satisfies no
one and is, nonetheless, correct.

---

### Vetr

**Dagmál:** Winter. Endurance. Survival under the weight of cold.
Vetr is carved on storehouse doors and winter-camp markers and
the walking-staves of people who travel the Long Dark.

**Náttmál:** The season that does not end. Patient ruin. Night-
carved Vetr on a settlement's well means winter touches the
water — not ice, but cold that persists into the Long Summer.
The crops falter. The ground stays hard. The livestock huddle.
Vetr náttmál is the Veil's favorite rune, and the galdr-workers
know this, and they carve it last and erase it first and they
handle it the way a smith handles a crucible of molten iron:
quickly, carefully, and with deep respect for what happens
when it spills.

---

## Galdr Vocalization — The Throat-Singing

The rune carved into material is half the working. The other
half is the voice.

Galdr is not spoken. It is not sung in the way a skald sings or
a mother hums a lullaby. Galdr is _throat-voiced_ — a guttural,
resonant, compressed vibration that originates in the chest and
exits the throat as something between speech and growl. The
galdr-worker does not choose the pitch. The pitch chooses itself,
rising from the gut the way the rune's shape rises from the
material. The correct tone is the one that makes the carved line
glow. The incorrect tone is the one that makes the carver's nose
bleed.

The old galdr manuals — fragments survive at Skaldhaven —
describe the voice as _rúnamunnr_ (rune-mouth): the physical
apparatus that translates carved symbol into activated power.
The carving is the letter. The voice is the breath that makes
the letter a word. Without the voice, the rune is dormant. With
the wrong voice, the rune inverts. With the right voice, the
rune remembers what it was meant to do and does it, and the
world around the carver shifts to accommodate the demand.

### What the Singing Does to the Singer

The physical effects accumulate. Short-term, a galdr-working
leaves the singer's throat raw, the voice hoarse, and the chest
aching as though the ribs have been compressed. The taste of
copper persists for hours. The breath clouds even in warm air,
as though the body is exhaling something colder than the
surrounding temperature.

Long-term, the throat changes. A galdr-worker of ten years has a
speaking voice that carries a permanent undertone — not a pitch,
but a texture, as though the vocal cords have developed a second
resonance that runs beneath the normal voice. People hear it
without identifying it. It makes them uneasy. Children cry.
Dogs lower their ears and back away. The galdr-worker sounds
_inhabited_ — as though the voice is not entirely one voice.

At rank 4 and above, the throat-singing produces involuntary
effects on listeners. The galdr is not aimed at them — the rune
is the target, the carving is the focus — but the vibration
spills. The overflow manifests as:

**Dark emotional resonance.** Listeners within earshot
experience a sudden, sourceless shift in mood. Not fear
exactly — deeper. A memory of grief surfaces without trigger. An
old anger flares without cause. The feeling is
indistinguishable from genuine emotion, and that is the danger:
the listeners believe the grief is theirs, the anger is theirs,
and they act on it. A hall full of armed men hearing galdr at
rank 4 may erupt into violence not because the galdr targeted
them but because the sound woke something in each man that was
already there, waiting for permission.

**Compulsive joining.** The most disturbing effect. At rank 4
and above, some listeners begin to sing along. Not consciously.
The throat vibrates. The chest resonates. A low hum emerges from
the listener's body before the listener's mind has agreed to
produce it. The hum harmonizes with the galdr — not perfectly,
but enough. In a room of twelve people, three or four will begin
humming. They cannot stop until the galdr-worker stops. They do
not know they are doing it until someone tells them, and when
told, they cannot explain why their body obeyed a sound their
mind did not authorize.

**Physical arousal.** The galdr's resonance moves through the
body. At certain frequencies — the náttmál register, the low
growl that activates the dark-face runes — the vibration
triggers involuntary physical responses. Pulse accelerates.
Skin flushes. Sexual arousal manifests without stimulus,
without desire, without the listener's consent or participation.
It is a body responding to a frequency the way a tuning fork
responds to its note — mechanically, impersonally, and with no
regard for the person inside the body. This effect is most
pronounced during extended workings (thirty minutes or longer)
and most dangerous during group-galdr, when multiple voices
produce harmonic interference that amplifies the base frequency.
The old manuals describe this as _holdboð_ — flesh-command —
and the galdr-workers who still know the term do not use it
casually.

**Numbness and pressure.** Listeners report tingling in the
extremities — fingers, toes, lips — followed by numbness that
climbs. At thirty minutes of exposure, the hands are insensate.
At an hour, the arms. The galdr-worker himself feels this too —
the singing numbs the body from the outside in, as though the
vibration is replacing nerve-signal with rune-signal. A galdr-
worker who has been singing for two hours cannot feel his own
hands on the chisel. He carves by muscle memory and by the
rune's guidance, and if the rune decides his hand should slip,
his hand slips, and he does not feel the blade enter the palm.

**Blood pressure and the divine stroke.** The most severe
physical consequence. Extended galdr — rank 4 or 5 workings
that last hours — produces symptoms identical to acute
hypertension: pounding headache, visual disturbance, nausea,
chest pressure, ringing ears. The galdr-workers call this
_guðslag_ (god-stroke). It is not metaphorical. Galdr-workers
die of it. The throat-singing forces blood pressure upward
through a mechanism that the seiðr-wives say is spiritual
(the body trying to contain divine energy) and the galdr-
workers say is physical (the vibration constricting blood
vessels in the throat and chest). Both are probably right.

Guðslag at its worst produces hemorrhagic stroke. The galdr-
worker collapses mid-chant. One pupil dilates. The face goes
slack on one side. The rune on the stone glows brighter than
it should — brighter than any rune the witnesses have seen —
and then it goes dark, and the galdr-worker is dead or wishes
they were. Survivors of guðslag lose function on one side of
the body and lose access to the higher ranks of galdr
permanently. Their hands still know the shapes. Their throat
still knows the tones. The runes no longer answer. The body
paid and the runes took and the runes did not give back.

Three galdr-workers in the last century have died of guðslag
during rank 5 workings. Two more survived with permanent
disability. The Deepholm archive records each case with clinical
precision and does not editorialize, because the Deepholm
archivists understand that the data speaks for itself: the most
powerful magic in the Rimevegr kills the people who practice it,
and the mechanism is indistinguishable from natural disease, and
this is either coincidence or design, and neither answer is
comforting.

### Poetic Form — The Galdr Chant Structure

Every galdr working should have a voiced form: the _kvæði_
(verse-chant) that the galdr-worker speaks or growls while
carving. The kvæði is not decorative. It is the targeting system.
The rune carved into stone says _what_. The kvæði says _where_,
_when_, _how hard_, and _to whom_.

A well-formed kvæði has four elements:

1. **Name the rune.** Speak the rune's name aloud. This is the
   invocation — the act that tells the rune it is being called.
2. **State the image.** Give the rune a concrete anchor in the
   physical world: a place, a person, a thing. The more specific
   the image, the more precise the effect.
3. **State the change.** Say what the rune should do. Not in
   abstract terms — in physical, observable, sensory terms.
4. **Leave room.** The kvæði should not close every door. A line
   of ambiguity — a space for the rune to interpret — is the
   difference between galdr that works and galdr that works
   exactly as intended and nothing more.

The tone is guttural, compressed, rhythmic. The lines should
feel like they cost something to speak. The Rimevegr's kvæðir
sound like commands issued through clenched teeth — not because
the carver is angry, but because the voice is carrying weight
that vocal cords were not designed to carry.

### Kvæði Examples — Dagmál and Náttmál

Each rune's voiced form differs between the day-face and the
night-face. The dagmál kvæðir are steady, warm, earned. The
náttmál kvæðir are lower, slower, and they taste wrong in the
mouth — the galdr-worker feels the words pulling at something
beneath the tongue, as though the speech is being spoken from
the gut, not the throat.

**Fé dagmál:**

> "Fé in the fold, fé in the hand. Let the cattle know their
> land."

**Fé náttmál:**

> "Fé in the rot, fé in the bone. What you hoarded eats alone."

**Úr dagmál:**

> "Úr in the bone, Úr in the breath. Stand like the bull against
> decline."

**Úr náttmál:**

> "Úr in the sinew, Úr in the blind. The beast survives. The
> man stays behind."

**Þurs dagmál:**

> "Þurs on the edge, thorn on the door. No hungry thing crosses
> here."

**Þurs náttmál:**

> "Þurs in the wall, Þurs in the skin. Nothing leaves. Nothing
> gets in."

**Kaun dagmál:**

> "Kaun in the coal, Kaun in the crack. Let the fire cut what
> must be cut."

**Kaun náttmál:**

> "Kaun in the loved, Kaun in the kept. What you treasure burns
> while you slept."

**Óss dagmál:**

> "Óss on the tongue, Óss in the ear. Let truth arrive and
> answer."

**Óss náttmál:**

> "Óss in the jaw, Óss in the bone. Your mouth speaks truths
> you'd leave alone."

**Reið dagmál:**

> "Reið on the road, Reið in the hoof. Carry us where the path
> agrees."

**Reið náttmál:**

> "Reið in the circle, Reið in the blind. Walk until your legs
> remind."

**Gjöf dagmál:**

> "Gjöf for gjöf, hand for hand. Let trust return as it was
> given."

**Gjöf náttmál:**

> "Gjöf in the chain, gjöf in the need. The gift that feeds
> becomes the feed."

**Vend dagmál:**

> "Vend in the hearth, Vend in the smile. Let hard hearts
> remember peace."

**Vend náttmál:**

> "Vend in the lull, Vend in the sweet. Sleep warm. Sleep deaf.
> Sleep through the feet."

**Hagall dagmál:**

> "Hagall in the air, Hagall on the skin. Break the lying shape
> of things."

**Hagall náttmál:**

> "Hagall in the rafter, Hagall in the year. Everything you
> built cracks here."

**Nauð dagmál:**

> "Nauð in the chest, Nauð in the stone. Endure until the way
> opens."

**Nauð náttmál:**

> "Nauð in the throat, Nauð in the empty. Need without name.
> Need without plenty."

**Íss dagmál:**

> "Íss on the river, Íss on the lid. Still what must not move."

**Íss náttmál:**

> "Íss in the voice, Íss in the bed. Freeze the living. Quiet
> the dead."

**Ár dagmál:**

> "Ár in the field, Ár in the hand. Let the year give back its
> due."

**Ár náttmál:**

> "Ár in the debt, Ár in the ground. The year comes due. The
> year comes round."

**Dagur dagmál:**

> "Dagur on the brow, Dagur on the road. Bring the hidden thing
> into day."

**Dagur náttmál:**

> "Dagur in the sleep, Dagur on the tongue. Your secrets walk
> before the sun."

**Yr dagmál:**

> "Yr in the bow, Yr in the grave. Let the mark fly true and
> final."

**Yr náttmál:**

> "Yr in the step, Yr in the luck. The arrow's loosed. You
> cannot duck."

**Peorð dagmál:**

> "Peorð in the fall, Peorð in the turn. Let the hidden choice
> reveal itself."

**Peorð náttmál:**

> "Peorð in the cup, Peorð in the cheat. Every borrowed win
> pays double in defeat."

**Óðal dagmál:**

> "Óðal in the ground, Óðal in the name. Let the right belong
> to the right."

**Óðal náttmál:**

> "Óðal in the blood, Óðal in the sin. The land remembers what
> your line has been."

**Elgur dagmál:**

> "Elgur on the antler, Elgur on the hill. Carry us clear of
> the snare."

**Elgur náttmál:**

> "Elgur on the door, Elgur on the lock. The shelter holds.
> The shelter does not stop."

**Sól dagmál:**

> "Sól on the face, Sól on the wood. Make the true shape
> impossible to hide."

**Sól náttmál:**

> "Sól in the wound, Sól in the eye. No shadow. No rest. No
> place to lie."

**Týr dagmál:**

> "Týr on the oath, Týr on the hand. Let justice remember the
> blade."

**Týr náttmál:**

> "Týr in the grip, Týr in the turn. The blade finds who the
> blade must learn."

**Bjarkan dagmál:**

> "Bjarkan in the bark, Bjarkan in the root. Grow again where
> the wound was."

**Bjarkan náttmál:**

> "Bjarkan in the flesh, Bjarkan in the wrong. The body heals.
> The body heals too long."

**Maður dagmál:**

> "Maður in the hall, Maður in the law. Let human order stand
> together."

**Maður náttmál:**

> "Maður in the room, Maður in the gone. You stand among them.
> They stand on."

**Eykur dagmál:**

> "Eykur in the stride, Eykur in the cart. Carry the burden
> farther than before."

**Eykur náttmál:**

> "Eykur in the rein, Eykur in the red. The horse runs north.
> The rider's dead."

**Lögur dagmál:**

> "Lögur in the depth, Lögur in the mirror. Let memory flow
> and show."

**Lögur náttmál:**

> "Lögur in the cup, Lögur in the drown. Drink and remember
> what pulled you down."

**Ing dagmál:**

> "Ing in the seed, Ing in the bed. Let peace and fruitfulness
> remain."

**Ing náttmál:**

> "Ing in the womb, Ing in the price. The child survives. The
> rest is sacrifice."

---

## Rune-Script Construction — The Grammar of Galdr

Rune magic is a language. Each rune is a word. Combining runes
creates a sentence, and the sentence is the spell.

### Script Size and Power

The number of runes in a script determines its scope:

- **3 runes:** A single, clean effect. One sentence. One demand.
  Most galdr in the Rimevegr is three-rune work.
- **5 runes:** A layered effect. Two sentences that reinforce
  each other. Rank 3+ galdr-workers use five-rune scripts for
  complex wards and bindings.
- **7 runes:** Major scene control. The galdr-worker is not
  asking the world to change — they are telling it. Rank 4
  minimum. The kvæði for a seven-rune script takes twenty
  minutes to chant.
- **9 runes:** Mythic-level working. The old sagas describe
  nine-rune scripts that sealed entire valleys, raised storms
  that lasted seasons, and bound the dead to obedience for
  generations. No living galdr-worker has successfully completed
  a nine-rune script. The last verified attempt was in year 247.
  The galdr-worker's name was Eiríkr. The script was meant to
  seal a barrow-field permanently. The galdr succeeded. Eiríkr
  died of guðslag before the final line was cut. His apprentice
  finished the chant. The barrow-field is still sealed. The
  apprentice cannot speak above a whisper and has not been able
  to since.

### Script Construction Pattern

Every script follows a grammar:

**Pattern 1 — Noun + Verb + Consequence:**

- `Fé + Óss + Gjöf` — Cattle obey the voice and return
  home through trust.
- `Kaun + Hagall + Nið` — Fire falls like a storm on a
  house of shame.
- `Yr + Mál + Hrafn` — The grave speaks through omen
  and carrion-sign.

**Pattern 2 — Object + Force + Outcome:**

- `Steinn + Nauð + Elgur` — Stone endures and becomes
  sanctuary.
- `Skuggi + Reið + Hlið` — Shadow travels and the gate
  opens one way.
- `Blóð + Týr + Nið` — Blood demands justice and the
  betrayer is shamed.

**Pattern 3 — Social + Natural:**

- `Gjöf + Ár + Vend` — Gift and harvest build household
  bliss.
- `Mál + Vetr + Nauð` — Law endures through winter's
  hardship.
- `Nið + Kaun + Hagall` — Shame burns and the storm
  takes what's left.

### Dark Script Examples — Rimevegr Workings

These are the galdr-scripts that galdr-workers whisper about
and Lawspeakers pretend do not exist.

#### 1. Seal the Dead Who Will Not Stay

Runes: Yr + Steinn + Elgur

Kvæði:

> "Yr in the bone, Steinn on the lid, Elgur at the mouth.
> What sleeps here stays sleeping. The stone remembers the
> weight."

Effect: The barrow-ward holds the dead in place. Standard
defensive galdr. Every settlement has at least one of these.
The dark part is not the script — the dark part is that they
need refreshing every twenty years now, and twenty years ago
it was thirty, and thirty years before that it was fifty.

#### 2. Burn the Oath-Breaker's Hall

Runes: Kaun + Nið + Óðal

Kvæði:

> "Kaun in the rafter, Nið on the name, Óðal in the ash.
> Let the fire know what the line has done. Let the land
> forget the house."

Effect: The hall burns. The fire targets the structure, not the
people — but the people are inside the structure, and the fire
does not distinguish between the two after the first minute.
The land where the hall stood will not support a building for
a generation. The family name becomes a cautionary tale.

#### 3. Curse the Betrayer's Blood

Runes: Blóð + Týr + Nið + Ár + Óðal

Kvæði:

> "Blóð on the oath, Týr in the breach, Nið on the tongue,
> Ár in the debt, Óðal in the line. The blood remembers. The
> law remembers. The land remembers. The year comes due."

Effect: A five-rune curse-script. The betrayer bleeds for the
broken oath. The shame follows the family. The land records the
transgression. The year brings the reckoning. This is rank 4
galdr — expensive, dangerous, and devastating. The galdr-
worker who carves this pays 3 Willpower and 3 HP minimum, and
the script must be carved in the betrayer's blood (obtained
legally — a nick during a witnessed ting, a wound from an
honor-duel, a deliberate cut offered by the betrayer themselves
before they knew what the blood would be used for).

#### 4. Trap the Living Behind Their Own Walls

Runes: Hlið + Þurs + Mórk

Kvæði:

> "Hlið at the door, Þurs in the frame, Mórk at the line.
> The guest may enter. The guest may not leave. The boundary
> does the rest."

Effect: The building becomes a box. The door opens inward.
People can enter. No one exits until the rune is broken.
Used historically in three recorded cases: twice against
outlaws who occupied a settlement building, once against a
jarl's household during a coup. The third case is not
discussed in public because the jarl's household included
fourteen children, and the building was sealed for three days
before anyone broke the rune.

#### 5. Drown a Man in His Own Memories

Runes: Lögur + Nauð + Hrafn

Kvæði:

> "Lögur in the skull, Nauð in the need, Hrafn on the
> shoulder. The water rises. The raven watches. Drown in
> what you did."

Effect: The target remembers. Not selectively — totally. Every
cruelty they performed, every betrayal they committed, every
face they tried to forget. The memories arrive with full
sensory detail — the smell, the sound, the wet. The target
cannot eat, cannot sleep, cannot function until the memories
run their course, which takes between three days and three
weeks depending on how much the target has to remember. Two
men subjected to Lögur-Nauð-Hrafn scripts killed themselves
before the effect ended. The galdr-workers who carved the
scripts were acquitted at ting because the suicides were ruled
self-inflicted. The legal distinction between killing a man
and making him remember enough to kill himself is one of the
more disturbing features of Rimevegr jurisprudence.

#### 6. Erase a Man from His Community

Runes: Maður + Íss + Skuggi

Kvæði:

> "Maður in the gone, Íss in the voice, Skuggi on the face.
> Stand among them. They stand on."

Effect: Social erasure. The target is not invisible — he is
unremarkable. Present but unperceived. He speaks and the
words arrive but the urgency does not. He stands in a room
and the room forgets to include him. This is the rune-based
version of the outlaw's terminal loneliness described in
Chapter 14 — but achieved in minutes rather than years, and
inflicted from outside rather than grown from within.

#### 7. Make the Unborn Pay for the Father's Sin

Runes: Óðal + Blóð + Ár + Ing + Nið

Kvæði:

> "Óðal remembers the sin. Blóð carries the price. Ár turns
> the wheel. Ing bears the weight. Nið names the shame."

Effect: A five-rune generational curse. The most forbidden
galdr in the Rimevegr. Targets not the man but the man's line
— children, grandchildren, the family's future. The effects
are subtle and persistent: fertility drops. Livestock yield
decreases. Luck sours. The family's fortunes decline across
decades in ways that could be coincidence and are not. The
galdr-worker who carves this is legally niding if caught, and
no ting in the Rimevegr will hear a defense. The practice
survives because the evidence is indistinguishable from natural
misfortune, and because the families who suffer do not know a
rune was carved, and because the galdr-workers who carve them
do not talk.

### Bind-Runes — Permanent Workings

A bind-rune is a compound rune: two or more rune-forms
overlaid into a single carving. Where a script is a sentence,
a bind-rune is a single word made from multiple roots. The
binding compresses the effect but makes it permanent — or as
permanent as anything is in the Rimevegr, which means it lasts
until the material fails or the Veil eats the power.

**Ward bind-runes** — Protection and defense.

- `Þurs + Elgur + Úr` — Active defense, warding, bodily
  endurance. Kvæði: "Þurs at the flank, Elgur on the
  ridge, Úr in the ribs. Stand and do not break."
- `Mórk + Steinn + Salt` — Boundary sealed, permanent,
  purified. The standard barrow-ward. Kvæði: "Mórk at the
  edge, Steinn in the deep, Salt on the seal. The line
  holds. The dead sleep."

**Witness bind-runes** — Memory and truth.

- `Dagur + Mál + Óss` — Clarity, speech, authority. Carved
  on ting-stones. Kvæði: "Dagur sees, Mál measures, Óss
  speaks. The truth has no shade."
- `Hrafn + Yr + Steinn` — Omen, death, permanence. Carved
  on grave-markers to record how the dead died. Kvæði: "The
  raven saw. The yew remembers. The stone keeps."

**Burden bind-runes** — Curses and weight.

- `Nið + Blóð + Óðal` — Disgrace, blood-debt, lineage.
  The generational curse in compact form. Kvæði: "Nið on
  the house, Blóð on the name, Óðal remembers what the
  line has done."
- `Nauð + Vetr + Íss` — Need, winter, stillness. The
  binding of despair. Kvæði: "Need in the dark, winter in
  the bone, ice on the will. Wait. Wait. Wait."

**Battle bind-runes** — War and force.

- `Týr + Móðr + Úr` — Justice, fury, strength. The
  warrior's compound. Kvæði: "Týr in the hand, Móðr in
  the blood, Úr in the bone. Strike true and stand."
- `Kaun + Hagall + Þurs` — Fire, storm, thorn. The
  siege-breaker. Kvæði: "Kaun opens, Hagall breaks, Þurs
  punishes. Nothing holds."

---

## Rune-Blight — The Corruption of Galdr

The runes are not safe. They were never safe. The pre-Cracking
galdr-workers had divine patronage — the gods corrected the
pronunciation, steadied the hand, absorbed the overflow. Post-
Cracking galdr-workers have none of that. They carve with
orphaned tools and channel power through a system designed for
divine supervision that has been running unsupervised for three
centuries. The runes still work. They work wrong, sometimes.
They work _more than intended_, sometimes. And the wrongness
accumulates in the carver's body the way the cold accumulates
in the Rimevegr: slowly, specifically, and without mercy.

The galdr-workers call it _rúnavíti_ — rune-blight.

### What It Is

Rune-blight is the physical and mental corruption that results
from prolonged, excessive, or improper rune use. It is not the
same as the normal costs of galdr (the white hand-lines, the
numb fingertips, the permanent vocal undertone). Those costs
are the expected price. Rune-blight is what happens when the
price is not enough and the runes take more.

The distinction is cumulative exposure versus acute damage.
A galdr-worker who carves within their rank, rests between
workings, and respects the material's grain will pay the
standard costs and live to old age with strange hands and a
stranger voice. A galdr-worker who overreaches — carving above
rank, forcing runes into wrong materials, chanting náttmál
without understanding what the night-face wants — accumulates
blight.

### The Stages of Rune-Blight

**Stage 1 — The Tremor.** The galdr-worker's hands develop a
fine tremor that appears only during carving. The chisel
jumps. Lines waver. Simple runes that should be second nature
require concentration. The galdr-worker compensates by gripping
harder, which makes the tremor worse. At this stage, galdr
checks take a -5 penalty and the failure cascade shifts upward
by 10 on the table (see `20_SIMULATION_RULES.md`).

**Stage 2 — The Echo.** The runes begin to speak back. Not
audibly — the galdr-worker hears the rune's resonance in
peripheral attention, the way you hear a sound in a dream. The
echo is the rune's night-face offering opinions. A galdr-worker
carving Fé for a simple livestock blessing hears, beneath the
dagmál chant, the náttmál whisper: _what you own owns you_. The
echo cannot be silenced. It can be ignored, but ignoring it
requires Will checks, and each failure means the rune tilts
toward its dark face regardless of the carver's intent.

**Stage 3 — The Mark.** The blight becomes visible. The white
hand-lines that every galdr-worker develops begin to spread —
up the wrists, along the forearms. They are no longer thin.
They are raised, textured, and they glow faintly in the dark
with the same blue-white flicker as active rune-lines. The
galdr-worker's hands look like ward-stones. The galdr-worker
_is_ a ward-stone — the runes have decided that the carver's
body is a valid medium, and they are writing on it the same way
the carver writes on wood and bone and iron.

At this stage, the galdr-worker's presence begins to affect the
environment. Fires burn blue within arm's reach. Small metal
objects drift toward the hands. The air around the worker smells
of old iron permanently, not just during carving. Animals refuse
proximity. Other galdr-workers can feel the blight at a
distance — a pressure, a wrongness, the sensation of standing
too close to an active rune-stone.

**Stage 4 — The Speaking.** The galdr-worker's voice splits.
The permanent undertone that all experienced galdr-workers
develop separates into a distinct second voice — audible,
recognizable, and not the carver's. The second voice speaks
in rune-fragments: partial kvæðir, rune-names, the beginning
of chants the carver did not start and cannot stop. The
speaking happens during sleep, during conversation, during
silence. The galdr-worker is chanting constantly, beneath the
threshold of conscious control, and the chant is building
toward a working that the carver has not chosen and cannot
identify.

Other people hear it. The effect is the same as galdr overflow —
dark emotional resonance, compulsive humming, the physical
effects described above — but constant, low-level, unending.
Living with a Stage 4 galdr-worker is living inside a
permanent low-grade rune-working. The household adapts. The
household changes. The household stops noticing things that
visitors notice immediately: the blue fire-tips, the drifting
objects, the children who hum in their sleep.

**Stage 5 — The Rune Becomes the Person.** Terminal blight.
The galdr-worker's body completes its transformation into a
medium. The white lines cover the entire body — face, torso,
legs. The eyes develop the blue-white flicker. The voice is no
longer human in any recognizable sense; it is rune-speech,
constant, a low guttural chant that does not stop even when the
galdr-worker is unconscious. The person inside the body is still
present — this is not possession — but the person is now living
inside a rune-stone, and the rune-stone is active, and the
rune-stone is running a working that no one authorized.

No Stage 5 galdr-worker has been documented in the last century.
The Deepholm archive records three cases from the first century
post-Cracking, when galdr-workers pushed harder because the
failure of the old systems demanded more power than human bodies
could safely channel. All three were killed by their settlements
— not out of malice, but out of necessity. A Stage 5 galdr-
worker warps the environment in a radius of approximately
fifty paces. Within that radius, wards fail, the Veil thins,
the dead wake, and the living experience continuous galdr
overflow: dark emotions, compulsive humming, physical arousal,
numbness, headache. The settlement cannot function. The galdr-
worker cannot stop. The only recorded resolution is death, and
even then the body must be burned completely and the ashes
scattered in running water, because Stage 5 corpses have been
known to continue chanting for hours after the heart stops.

### Connection to the God-Scorned Path

The guðníðing described in Chapter 14 sometimes pursue rune-
blight deliberately. Where a normal galdr-worker accumulates
blight through overwork and overreach, the god-scorned carve
inverted runes into their own flesh, chant náttmál without
target or intent, and force the blight's progression as a form
of spiritual self-destruction.

The overlap between Stage 3 rune-blight (the Mark) and the
guðníðing's inverted ward-marks is not coincidental. Both
involve the runes writing on the carver's body. The difference
is direction: the blighted galdr-worker's marks are the runes'
initiative, carved from outside in. The guðníðing's marks are
self-inflicted, carved from inside out. The result is similar
but not identical. The blighted worker is being consumed by the
magic. The guðníðing is consuming themselves with the magic.

The three soulbanes described in Chapter 14 — mannát, dýrríða,
villimennska — interact with rune-blight in specific ways:

- **Mannát (the Eating)** kills the hugr. A galdr-worker whose
  hugr is dead cannot hear the rune's dagmál face. All galdr
  defaults to náttmál. The rune-blight accelerates because the
  brake is gone.
- **Dýrríða (the Riding)** kills the hamr. A galdr-worker
  whose hamr is dead has no barrier between the rune's will
  and the body. The speaking (Stage 4) arrives immediately.
  The runes write freely.
- **Villimennska (the Wilding)** kills the fylgja. A galdr-
  worker whose fylgja is dead has no divine inheritance, no
  residual connection to whatever the gods left behind. The
  galdr stops working entirely — not because the runes reject
  the carver, but because the carver has nothing left for the
  runes to channel through. A villimaðr cannot carve. The
  hands still move. The chisel still cuts. The runes are
  shapes on stone. They do not glow. They do not answer. The
  magic is gone.

This creates a bitter irony: the god-scorned who pursue rune-
blight as self-destruction find that the path of corruption
leads not to greater dark power but to the complete loss of
power. The runes are not tools of rebellion against the divine
order. They are fragments of the divine order, and destroying
the divine inheritance within oneself destroys access to the
fragments. The wolfshead who expected to become a dark galdr-
master becomes, instead, a man with scars on his hands and
silence where the runes used to speak.

_This is the Rimevegr's cruelest joke, and it is a joke nobody
tells because nobody finds it funny: the magic that is killing
the world is also the only thing keeping the world alive, and
the people who try to weaponize the killing find that the weapon
does not work without the thing it is destroying._

---

## The Phenomenology of Silence

Galdr is sung. The rune-carver opens his throat and the vibration
passes through the body into the wood. The sound is the channel.
The voice is the instrument. The body pays in blood and tremor,
and the work is audible — anyone standing near a galdr-worker at
full throat-song can hear the power moving through the air the way
they can hear wind through a crack in a wall. Galdr announces
itself. Galdr is loud.

Seiðr is the opposite.

When a seiðr-wife works, the world goes quiet. Not silent in the
way that a room is silent when no one speaks. Silent in the way
that a room is silent when something has eaten the sound. The fire
stops crackling. The timber stops settling. The wind outside dies
as if it has lost interest. The men in the room stop hearing their
own breathing, and it takes them several seconds to realize that
they have stopped — not that the breathing has stopped, but that
the sound of it has been swallowed. The seiðr-wife opens her mouth
and what comes out is not song. It is speech — flat, barren,
stripped of every quality that makes a voice recognizable as
belonging to a living person. The words are plain. The pace is
dull. The tone carries no warmth, no urgency, no music. And the
voice is not hers.

This is the defining phenomenology of seiðr: the practitioner
speaks, and the voice that emerges rides her lips like a traveller
riding a borrowed horse. The words are hers in the sense that her
mouth shapes them. They are not hers in any other sense. The pitch
is wrong. The cadence belongs to someone who breathed differently,
who formed vowels in a throat shaped by different years of use.
Sometimes the voice is recognizable — the client clutches the edge
of the bench because the dead man's wife just heard her husband's
pronunciation of a word he always said wrong, and the seiðr-wife
has never met the dead man, and the voice came through anyway.
Sometimes the voice belongs to no one present or remembered. It
is old, or foreign, or simply other, and the room cannot place
it, and the room is afraid.

The silence is not a side effect. The silence is the mechanism.

### Drawing the Veil

A seiðr-wife works by pulling the Veil toward herself. Not
through it, not against it — toward. The Veil is the boundary
between the living and the dead, and it has properties that
galdr-workers measure and seiðr-wives feel: it swallows sound,
muffles firelight, bends distance, drops the temperature until
breath fogs and fingertips go numb. These are the Veil's ambient
effects — the things it does when it is merely near.

When the seiðr-wife reaches, she draws those effects inward. She
becomes the centre of a localized Veil-thinning. The boundary
between the living and the dead does not weaken everywhere — it
weakens here, where she sits, in a radius measured in arm's-lengths
rather than miles. And as the Veil gathers around her, it
withdraws from the space around those who are watching.

This is why the silence feels wrong to the witnesses. The Veil is
being pulled away from them. Their world — the world of crackling
fire and settling timber and wind through cracks — becomes more
itself, not less. The sounds should be sharper. The fire should be
brighter. But the seiðr-wife is closer, and the Veil she has drawn
around herself radiates the silence outward like heat radiating
from a forge. The listeners are caught between two truths: the
world is louder because the Veil has retreated from them, and the
world is quieter because the person three paces away is wrapped in
a silence so complete that it leaks.

The leak is the seiðr-wife's working. She draws the Veil close
so that the dead can speak through the thinned boundary. The dead
do not shout. They do not need to. The Veil around the
practitioner is so thin that a whisper on the other side arrives
as clear speech on this one. And the silence is the cost — the
Veil swallows the living sound around the practitioner to make
room for the dead sound that comes through. It is not that the
seiðr-wife creates silence. It is that the Veil, gathered tight,
does what the Veil always does. It eats the noise of the living
world, and in the space it makes, the dead have room to speak.

A galdr-worker hearing the hum of rune-channels is hearing the
residue of divine infrastructure. A seiðr-wife hearing the dead
is hearing what the Veil carries on its other face. These are
not the same phenomenon. They share a source — the Cracking broke
both — but the channels are different. The runes were built. The
Veil was always there. The runes are failing. The Veil is not.

This is why seiðr has not weakened since the Cracking. The dead
are easier to reach now, not harder. The Veil thins a little more
each decade. The seiðr-wives' theory — the Devouring — holds that
something is eating the barrier from the other side, thinning it,
making the dead more accessible and the living world more
permeable. If they are right, then the seiðr-wife who draws the
Veil toward herself is not performing a difficult act. She is
performing an easy one, made easier by three centuries of
progressive decay. She is stepping into a river that is already
flowing downhill.

### The Silence in the Room

The silence has stages. Witnesses who have attended multiple
seiðr-workings describe a consistent progression, and the
progression is important because it maps the depth of the
practitioner's reach.

**Stage one: the dampening.** The fire quiets. The timber stops
its settling-sounds. Conversation becomes effortful — not because
speaking is harder, but because the acoustic space has changed. A
voice that carried across the room a moment ago now seems to stop
a pace from the speaker's mouth. People lean forward. They repeat
themselves. They notice, without being able to articulate it, that
the room has become smaller in a way that has nothing to do with
the walls.

**Stage two: the smothering.** Body sounds disappear. The
listener cannot hear their own breathing. They cannot hear their
heartbeat — not the heartbeat itself, which was never loud, but
the subliminal pulse that living bodies are accustomed to and do
not notice until it is gone. The absence is deeply unsettling. A
person who has never heard true silence — and no person living has,
because the body always makes noise — discovers what true silence
feels like, and it feels like death. The instinctive response is
to make noise: cough, shift weight, scrape a boot on the floor.
The noise is swallowed. The boot scrapes, but the scrape arrives
muffled, distant, as if it happened in another room. Panic begins
at stage two. Not the loud panic of a fight. The quiet panic of
a person who has realized that they are not entirely present in
their own body.

**Stage three: the arrest.** This is the stage that separates
casual observation from genuine seiðr-exposure. The witnesses
stop blinking. Not a decision — a physiological response. The
eyes fix on the seiðr-wife, dry and staring, and the witness
cannot look away. The visual field narrows. Peripheral objects
lose detail. The seiðr-wife's face, hands, and mouth become the
only things in focus, and the focus is so absolute that the
witness's eyes begin to ache from not blinking and they do not
blink anyway. The eyelids have stopped responding to voluntary
command.

Simultaneously, the sensation of time collapses. Five minutes
feels like thirty seconds or three hours, and the witness cannot
tell which. The fire has not moved — has it? The shadows have not
shifted — have they? The room exists in a single, extended present
tense that has no past and no future, only the seiðr-wife's mouth
and the words that come from it in a voice that does not belong to
her.

**Stage four: the stillness.** The witness's body goes quiet. Not
relaxed — arrested. The muscles hold their last position. The
hands do not move. The jaw does not clench or unclench. The chest
stops its visible rise and fall because the breathing has become
so shallow that the diaphragm barely moves. In some cases — the
well-documented cases, the ones the seiðr-wives themselves talk
about in careful, clinical terms — the witness stops breathing
entirely. Not a held breath. A cessation. The autonomous nervous
system, which has maintained respiratory rhythm since birth without
requiring conscious direction, simply pauses. The body forgets to
breathe.

This is not magic acting on the witness. This is the Veil's
proximity effect, amplified by the seiðr-wife's drawing. The Veil
does not distinguish between the practitioner and the audience.
It swallows what is near, and what is near includes the living
processes of everyone in the room. The dampening that took the
crackle from the fire takes the rhythm from the lungs. The
smothering that ate the heartbeat's subliminal pulse eats the
autonomic drive to inhale. The body does not resist because the
body does not know it should — the silence has already removed the
sensory cues that would trigger alarm.

The result is lightheadedness. Then dizziness. Then, if the
seiðr-working continues at depth, loss of consciousness. A witness
who faints during a seiðr-working has not fainted from fear. They
have fainted from hypoxia — oxygen deprivation caused by the
cessation of breathing that the Veil's silence induced. The
seiðr-wife did not do this to them. The seiðr-wife was focused on
the dead. The Veil did this to them because the Veil is not
selective and the seiðr-wife cannot control what the Veil does to
the people around her any more than a forge-worker can control
where the heat goes.

This is why seiðr-workings are attended by watchers — not guards,
but monitors whose job is to observe the audience and intervene
when someone stops breathing. The protocol is simple: shake the
person. Slap them if shaking fails. Drag them out of the room if
slapping fails. The fresh air and distance from the Veil's radius
restarts the breathing within seconds. The watcher does not need
to know why it works. They need to know that a person who has
stopped breathing in the seiðr-wife's presence will start again
if removed from the seiðr-wife's presence.

Dalla does not have watchers. The band does not know they are
needed. When Dalla works in camp — rarely, reluctantly, at Voss's
insistence when the contract requires it — the men sit around the
fire and watch, and they feel the silence press in, and they
attribute the dizziness to nerves and the lightheadedness to the
cold and the strange fixation on Dalla's mouth to something they
do not name. Nobody has fainted yet. Dalla has not yet worked at
a depth that would push stage four to its conclusion. But the
depth of her reach is increasing — seiðr strengthens with practice,
unlike galdr, which weakens — and the day will come when a
standard working pulls enough Veil into camp to stop someone's
breathing, and neither Dalla nor Voss will understand what
happened, because nobody told them this was coming.

### The Voice That Is Not Hers

The voice is the signature of seiðr-working, and it is the detail
that no witness forgets.

The seiðr-wife opens her mouth. The words that emerge are spoken
in a tone and register that do not match the woman who is
speaking them. The pitch may be higher or lower. The accent may
be regional, archaic, or unplaceable. The cadence is wrong — the
pauses fall in places where a living speaker would not pause, and
the emphasis lands on syllables that carry meaning in the dead
person's speech pattern, not in the seiðr-wife's.

The content is plain. No poetry, no drama, no elevation. The dead
speak the way they spoke in life, which means they speak the way
ordinary people speak: in fragments, in half-finished thoughts, in
the particular verbal habits they carried from birth to death and
apparently carry still. A farmer's dead husband talks about the
grain the way he always talked about the grain. A dead warrior
reports on his death with the flat, unelaborated directness of a
man who never learned to tell a story properly. The dead do not
become eloquent. They become more themselves — stripped of the
social filters that the living maintain, speaking without the
awareness that someone is listening and judging.

The seiðr-wife is not translating. She is not interpreting. She
is a surface — a membrane stretched thin enough that the dead
can press through and leave the imprint of their voice on the
living air. Her own awareness during the working varies by
practitioner and by depth. At shallow working — rank 1 and 2 —
the seiðr-wife is present. She hears what the dead say. She can
intervene, redirect, even refuse a spirit that is pushing too
hard or saying something the client should not hear. At deeper
working — rank 3 and above — the seiðr-wife recedes. Her
awareness narrows to a point and then extinguishes, and the dead
have the mouth, and the dead say what the dead want to say, and
the seiðr-wife comes back afterward with a headache and a gap in
her memory and asks the watcher what happened.

Dalla works at rank 3. She is aware during most of her workings,
but the awareness is thin — a candle flame in a wind that could
extinguish it at any time. She has come back from sessions with no
memory of the last third of the conversation. She has come back
and found the men staring at her with expressions she cannot
read — not fear exactly, but something adjacent to fear, something
that lives in the space between respect and revulsion. She does
not ask what she said. She has learned that the answer is never
comfortable.

The worst cases — the cases that seiðr-wives discuss among
themselves in quiet voices and never share with clients — are the
sessions where the voice does not leave. The seiðr-wife closes
the working. She pulls back from the Veil. She opens her eyes
and speaks, and the first words out of her mouth are in the dead
person's voice. Not because the spirit is still present. Because
the mouth has been shaped by the dead person's speaking for an
hour, and the muscles and the breath and the tongue have learned
a pattern that is not the seiðr-wife's, and the pattern does not
release immediately. It takes minutes. Sometimes hours. The
seiðr-wife speaks to her student and the student flinches because
the voice is the dead man's voice, and the seiðr-wife clears her
throat and tries again, and the voice is still wrong, and the
wrongness sits in the room like a cold spot that will not warm.

Ragnhild at Icebreak has a protocol for this. After every working,
she hums. Not a song — a hum, a single tone, held for as long as
her breath allows. She says the humming resets the throat. "The
dead borrow the mouth," she tells her students. "The hum takes
it back. If you do not take it back, the mouth forgets who owns
it." Her students hum after every session. The sound is thin and
unremarkable and it is the most important thing they do.

## The Cost of Silence

Galdr kills with fire. The rune-song builds in the throat until
the blood vessels strain and the heart labours and the stroke comes
like a fist behind the eyes. A galdr-worker who pushes too far
blows his heart singing. It is violent, immediate, and terminal.
The body burns from the inside. The man who dies of galdr backlash
dies loudly.

Seiðr does not kill like that. Seiðr does not kill loudly at all.

### The Slow Suffocation

The cost of seiðr is measured in oxygen. Not metaphorically — the
Veil's silence effect suppresses the autonomous breathing reflex,
and the suppression is cumulative. A single working of moderate
depth leaves the seiðr-wife with a headache and the feeling of
having held her breath too long. Two workings in a day — a pace
that contract demands sometimes require — leave her grey-faced
and unsteady, with the specific dizziness that comes from blood
that has not carried enough air. Three workings in a day has
happened twice in documented seiðr history. Both practitioners
collapsed. One recovered. One did not.

The mechanism is the Veil's ambient effect on the practitioner
herself. She draws the Veil close. The Veil suppresses sound,
and sound includes the feedback loops that the body uses to
regulate breathing. The seiðr-wife in trance breathes at a rate
that would concern a healer if the healer were monitoring — four
or five breaths per minute instead of twelve to fifteen. The
breaths are shallow. The chest barely moves. The body is in a
state of controlled oxygen deprivation that the trance makes
tolerable the way cold makes frostbite tolerable — by numbing
the signals that would otherwise force a correction.

Over years, the accumulated hypoxic episodes leave marks. Seiðr-
wives who have practiced for two decades show cognitive slowing —
not dramatic, not disabling, but measurable in the pauses between
question and answer, in the moments when the word they want is
on the other side of a gap that was not there five years ago.
Their sleep is poor, not because of nightmares (though the
nightmares come too) but because the respiratory drive has been
trained by years of suppression to operate at a lower baseline.
They breathe less deeply asleep than other people. They wake more
often, gasping, because the body dropped below the oxygen
threshold and the survival reflex overrode the trained
suppression.

This is the slow cost. Not the dramatic cost — not the stroke
that kills the galdr-worker mid-song, not the spectacle of a
man dying with rune-fire in his throat. The slow cost is a
woman who breathes a little less each year, who thinks a little
slower each decade, who sleeps a little worse each winter, who
will die at fifty-five of something the healer calls pneumonia or
heart weakness but which is actually the sum total of ten thousand
trance-sessions in which the Veil ate the air from her lungs and
she did not fight it because fighting it would have broken the
working.

### Physical Markers

The galdr-worker's cost is written in white scars. The
seiðr-wife's cost is written in what is missing.

**Complexion.** Active seiðr-wives develop a pallor that does not
respond to sun or diet. The skin takes on a blue-grey undertone
— not dramatic enough to alarm a casual observer, but visible to
anyone who knew the woman before she began serious practice. The
pallor is circulatory: years of low-oxygen trance-states produce
chronic low-grade anemia and reduced peripheral perfusion. The
body learns to route blood to the core, protecting the organs at
the expense of the skin. Dalla is twenty-eight. She has been pale
since she was twenty-three. The men in the band attribute it to
the cold. It is not the cold.

**Temperature sensitivity.** The seiðr-wife runs cold. Not the
temporary chill of Veil-proximity — a permanent, baseline shift
in body temperature that seiðr-wives notice in themselves and
their students notice in their teachers. Hands that are always
cool. Feet that do not warm in bedding. A preference for sitting
closest to the fire that is not social but physiological. The
Veil's cold follows the practitioner out of the trance the way
the galdr-worker's tremor follows the carver away from the bench.

**Voice changes.** A seiðr-wife's natural voice changes over the
course of her practice. Not from the dead — from the Veil itself.
The repeated suppression of normal vocal patterns during
trance-work, combined with the months and years of speaking in
voices that are not her own, produces a voice that becomes
increasingly flat. The music leaves. The inflection narrows. The
woman who laughed with a bright, carrying sound at twenty laughs
with a low, level sound at thirty-five, and by forty she does not
laugh at all — not from joylessness, but because the vocal
apparatus has been so thoroughly colonized by dead voices and
Veil-silence that the spontaneous sounds of living expression no
longer come naturally. She must choose to inflect. She must
decide to vary her tone. The effort is small but constant, and
when she is tired, she stops making the effort, and the voice
that comes out is the voice of someone who is not entirely here.

**Nail and lip color.** The chronic low oxygen shows in the
extremities. Fingernails and toenails develop a persistent
blue-grey tinge. Lips are darker than they should be — not
blue, not obviously cyanotic, but a shade deeper than healthy
tissue. These are the details that a trained healer would notice
and a layperson would not, and they are the details that other
seiðr-wives read instantly when meeting a colleague. The color
of the nails tells the story of the practice: how deep, how
often, how long.

### Seiðr Is Not Terminal the Way Galdr Is Terminal

The critical distinction: galdr can kill you in a single session.
The guðslag — the god-stroke — is a catastrophic event. The
rune-song builds until the heart cannot sustain it, and the
practitioner receives what amounts to a massive stroke as the
price of pushing the throat-song past what the body can channel.
The galdr-worker who "blows his heart singing" dies on the floor
with rune-fire in his veins and the last carved mark unfinished
on the wood. It is violent. It is immediate. It is the
temptation that every galdr-worker carries: to push the song one
rank higher, to feel the full resonance one more time, to die in
the work rather than fade from it.

Seiðr offers no such temptation. There is no single session that
kills. There is no dramatic peak, no moment of transcendent risk,
no glorious self-destruction. Seiðr kills the way water kills
stone — by persistence, by repetition, by the slow accumulation
of damage that is too small to notice in any single instance and
too large to survive across a lifetime. A seiðr-wife does not
blow her heart. She empties her lungs, one trance at a time, one
breath at a time, until the lungs have been emptied so many times
that they forget how to fill.

This means that seiðr practitioners live longer than galdr-workers,
on average. They are not killed by their art in their thirties or
forties the way the most active galdr-workers are. They die in
their fifties and sixties, of conditions that look natural and are
natural — pneumonia, heart failure, the simple failure of a body
that has run on reduced oxygen for decades. The deaths are quiet.
The deaths are unremarkable. The settlement buries the old
seiðr-wife and says she died of age, and the healer writes
nothing unusual in the record, and nobody counts the ten thousand
trances that took ten thousand breaths that took ten years from
the back end of a life that was never going to be long enough.

The seiðr-wives know. They count. Not formally — there is no
ledger, no archive. They count in the way that people count who
have watched their teachers die: Ragnhild watched her teacher
die at fifty-one. Ragnhild is sixty-two. She knows what the math
means. She does not discuss the math with her students because the
math is the same for all of them and discussing it changes nothing.
The practice kills. The practice is necessary. The dead need to
be spoken with and the Veil needs to be navigated and the silence
is the price and the price is breath and breath is life measured
in litres of air that the lungs did not take when the Veil said
not to.

## The Inversion: Seiðr and the Wolfsheads

The seiðr-wife draws the Veil toward herself, and the Veil eats
the sound around her, and the listeners go still, and the room
falls silent.

The wolfshead walks into the waste, and the Veil presses toward
him uninvited, and the sound of his own body becomes deafening.

These are the same force. The same boundary. The same mechanism.
The direction is inverted.

### Why the Outlaw Hears His Own Breathing

Chapter 14 describes the Veil-Pull — the gravitational inclination
that draws outlaws inward, toward the ice, toward the thinning.
The Pull is progressive. First the mood darkens. Then the anger
arrives. Then the mind reorganizes around Veil-logic. Then the
loneliness, and then the walk into the fog from which there is
no return.

But before all of that — before the depression, before the
violence, before the structural madness — there is a symptom that
every outlaw reports and no outlaw understands. It is the oldest
description in the wolfshead tradition, predating the formal
documentation of the Veil-Pull by generations:

_The breathing is too loud._

Not metaphor. Not anxiety. The outlaw, alone in the waste, begins
to hear his own breathing with a clarity that is physiologically
impossible under normal conditions. The inhale is a roar. The
exhale is a gale. The heartbeat is a drum struck by a fist. The
blood in the ears is a river. The body, which has been silent
background noise for every moment of the outlaw's life, becomes
the loudest thing in the world.

This is the Veil doing what the Veil does — but in the wrong
direction.

When the seiðr-wife draws the Veil close, she pulls it away from
the listeners. Their world gets quieter because the Veil's
sound-eating property has been focused on the practitioner. The
listeners lose the fire-crackle and the timber-settle and their
own heartbeat, but they lose it because the Veil has retreated
from them and gathered around the seiðr-wife.

When the Veil presses toward the outlaw, it does not retreat from
anything. It gathers around a person who has not invited it, who
has no training to manage it, who does not understand what is
happening. And the Veil does not eat the outlaw's body sounds.
It amplifies them. Because the Veil is a boundary, and when a
boundary presses against the living without the living's consent,
it does not create silence. It creates noise. The boundary
vibrates. The living body, caught in the Veil's pressure, becomes
an instrument — resonating with the friction between the world of
the living and the world of the dead.

The outlaw hears his breathing because the Veil is pressed so
tight against his living body that every process of life —
breathing, blood flow, the electrical crackle of nerve impulse,
the creak of tendon and joint — is amplified by the boundary's
proximity. He is not going deaf. He is not imagining things. He
is hearing himself die by inches, because the Veil is slowly
converting his living sounds into dead ones, and the conversion
is noisy.

The Rimevegr has no word for tinnitus. It has the phrase
_andaþrumur_ — breath-thunder. The outlaws use it. The
seiðr-wives recognize it. It is the archaic description of a
condition that, in a world with different medical knowledge, would
be diagnosed as severe, progressive tinnitus caused by chronic
Veil exposure — the boundary pressing against the inner ear, the
auditory nerve, the bone conduction pathways that the body uses to
hear itself. The thunder is not a metaphor. It is the sound of a
living body resonating with a force that was designed to separate
the living from the dead, and the resonance gets louder the longer
the exposure continues.

### The Symmetry

The seiðr-wife and the wolfshead are mirror images.

The seiðr-wife invites the Veil. The wolfshead receives it
uninvited. The seiðr-wife has training, protocol, herb-craft,
and the discipline of a tradition that has survived three
centuries of the Cracking. The wolfshead has nothing — no
training, no understanding, no protection except the salt he
carries and the iron at his belt.

The seiðr-wife's silence radiates outward. The room goes quiet.
The listeners stop breathing. The effect is focused, bounded,
contained within the space of the working. When the working ends,
the Veil releases. Sound returns. Breathing resumes. The damage is
to the seiðr-wife, not to the room.

The wolfshead's noise is inward. The waste is silent. The outlaw
is deafening — to himself. The breath-thunder fills his skull
and he cannot stop it because there is no working to end, no
trance to close, no protocol that pulls the Veil away. The Veil
is not being drawn. It is advancing. And it will not retreat
because the outlaw asks it to. It will retreat when the outlaw
returns to the company of the living — to a settlement, to a
band, to any concentration of human presence that makes the Veil's
advance less efficient than its persistence. Alone, the outlaw is
the easiest target the Veil has. The boundary presses closer each
night. The breathing gets louder each week. The thunder in the
skull gets worse until the outlaw cannot sleep, cannot think,
cannot hear anything except the sound of his own dying body, and
then the Walk begins — the slow, deliberate movement toward the
ice, toward the thinning, where the Veil will finally close over
him and the noise will finally stop.

The seiðr-wives know this. Not from theory — from practice. A
seiðr-wife who works without adequate recovery time begins to
hear the breath-thunder between sessions. It is the same sound.
The same mechanism. The Veil, drawn too often and too close, does
not fully retreat when the trance ends. Residual proximity remains.
The seiðr-wife hears her own breathing too loudly in the quiet
hours before sleep, and she knows what it means, and she adjusts
her practice schedule, and she waits for the residual to fade. It
always fades. So far. But each seiðr-wife who has practiced long
enough knows a colleague for whom it stopped fading — a woman who
began hearing the breath-thunder at twenty-five and who hears it
still at forty, muted but present, a sound that was supposed to
be temporary and became permanent, a reminder that the Veil does
not forget who drew it close and does not forgive the presumption.

Ragnhild hears it. She has heard it for fifteen years. She does
not discuss it with her students because the discussion would
require admitting that the practice she teaches will, given enough
time, produce the same symptom that kills the outlaws who hear it
without training. The difference is degree. The outlaw hears
thunder. Ragnhild hears a whisper. But the whisper has been there
for fifteen years and it has not faded, and Ragnhild is honest
enough with herself to know that the whisper is the same sound as
the thunder, heard from further away, and the distance is not
growing.

## Seiðr and Gender

Seiðr is women's work. This is not cultural preference. This is
not arbitrary tradition. This is the observation of three centuries
of practice, confirmed by every seiðr-wife who has ever trained a
student: the Veil responds to women differently than it responds
to men, and the difference matters.

### Why Women

The Veil is drawn by stillness. Not physical stillness — a galdr-
worker can be motionless and the Veil does not come. The stillness
the Veil responds to is internal: a quieting of the hugr, the
mind-soul, that allows the boundary to approach without the
boundary interpreting the approach as a threat. The seiðr-wife
does not reach through the Veil. She invites the Veil to reach
through her. The difference is the difference between kicking
down a door and opening it.

Women, in the Rimevegr's observation, achieve this internal
stillness more readily than men. The seiðr tradition attributes
this to the hugr's construction — a woman's hugr, shaped by
the experience of carrying and losing children in a land that
kills half of them before their fifth winter, learns early to
hold grief without breaking, to be still in the presence of
death without flinching from it. The Veil reads this stillness
as permission. The dead, pressing against the boundary, feel
the invitation and respond.

A man's hugr, shaped by the requirements of survival in the
Rimevegr — the violence, the posturing, the constant readiness
that the culture demands of anyone who carries a weapon — is loud.
Not noisy in the audible sense. Loud in the way the Veil reads it:
aggressive, defended, closed. The Veil does not approach a closed
hugr. It presses against it, and the friction produces not silence
but noise — the breath-thunder, the amplification, the
resonance that the outlaws experience. A man who attempts seiðr
is a man whose hugr is fighting the Veil's approach even as his
training tells him to accept it, and the fight produces a working
that is louder, rougher, less controlled, and more dangerous than
the same working performed by a woman whose hugr opens like a
door.

### The Ergi Question

A man who practices seiðr has done something the culture cannot
forgive: he has made his hugr still. He has quieted the part of
himself that the Rimevegr defines as masculine — the readiness,
the defended posture, the closed gate that says _nothing enters
here without my permission_. He has opened the gate. He has
invited the dead through. And the culture, which understands
gates and their opening in deeply gendered terms, reads this
opening as a surrender of masculinity itself.

The ergi stigma is not about sexuality. It is about
uncontrollability. A man who will open his hugr to the dead has
demonstrated a willingness to dissolve the boundary that defines
him as a man in this culture. If he will dissolve that boundary,
what other boundaries will he dissolve? If he will let the dead
speak through his mouth, whose else's words will he carry? If he
has made himself a door, who else will walk through?

The stigma is political, not moral. A man who practices seiðr
cannot be trusted with the responsibilities that the culture
assigns to men — oath-keeping, boundary-defense, the maintenance
of the closed, defended self that the Rimevegr requires of its
warriors and its jarls. He has become something else. Something
that the culture does not have a category for, and what the
culture cannot categorize, the culture destroys.

Male seiðr-practitioners exist. They practice in secret, or they
practice in exile, or they practice within a band like Voss's
where the rules are looser and the silence is maintained by
mutual agreement. Thorne's careful framing of his rune-work as
"reading" rather than "seeing" is the linguistic dodge that every
male practitioner learns — the words that keep the ergi question
at arm's length, the terminology that allows a man to use what he
has without admitting what he is.

## Seiðr as Intelligence Work

The political dimension of seiðr is not its spiritual function —
speaking with the dead, reading the wyrd, healing the wounded. The
political dimension is information.

A seiðr-wife who can speak with the dead can speak with anyone's
dead. A jarl's murdered rival. A merchant's drowned partner. The
previous holder of a disputed land-claim. The dead do not respect
confidentiality agreements. The dead do not maintain the fictions
that the living construct to govern property, inheritance, and
